            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Transition metal carbides are an emerging class of anode materials for Li-ion batteries (LIBs), which have recently drawn attention because of their good cond. and high capacity after rational nano-engineering.  In this work, we have developed Mo2C/N-doped carbon mesoporous heteronanowires (Mo2C/N-C MHNWs) with enhanced capacitive behavior as high-performance anode materials for LIBs.  With the heterostructure, the Mo2C nanocrystallites offer short paths for Li+ diffusion, while the N-doped carbon matrix facilitates fast electron transportation and buffers the vol. change of Mo2C during the discharge/charge cycles.  When evaluated as anodes for LIBs, the Mo2C/N-C MHNWs exhibited high capacity and high rate capability, as well as a long-term cycle life.  In particular, a reversible capacity of 744.6 mA h g-1 was achieved in the first cycle, and 732.9 mA h g-1 was preserved after 700 cycles at a c.d. of 2 A g-1.  The outstanding performance stems from fast kinetics enhanced by the pseudocapacitive effect, which was evidenced in the further anal. based on electrochem. impedance spectra and cyclic voltammetry.  Our results elucidate the attractive Li+ storage performance of Mo2C-based nanocomposites, which may shed some light on the development of high-performance materials for energy storage and utilization.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo--0mlN9av-rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lg7ifz8L3ww4g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28Xptl2rtrg%253D&amp;md5=e31c194ccc948234d67ceea124726758</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref62"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref62'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">62</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit62" data-doi="10.1016/j.actamat.2007.12.002"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hirai, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ichitsubo, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Uda, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Miyazaki, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yagi, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Matsubara, E.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Effects of Volume Strain Due to Li-Sn Compound Formation on Electrode Potential in Lithium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Acta Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2008</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>56</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1539</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">1545</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1359-6454</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">By applying the shape memory effect of the NiTi alloys to buffer the Sn anodes, demonstrate a simple approach to overcome a long-standing challenge of Sn anode in the applications of Li-ion batteries - the capacity decay.  By supporting the Sn anodes with NiTi shape memory alloys, the large vol. change of Sn anodes due to lithiation and delithiation can be effectively accommodated, based on the stress-induced martensitic transformation and superelastic recovery of the NiTi matrix resp., which leads to a decrease in the internal stress and closing of cracks in Sn anodes.  Accordingly, stable cycleability (630 mA·h·g-1 after 100 cycles at 0.7C) and excellent high-rate capabilities (478 mA·h·g-1 at 6.7C) were attained with the NiTi/Sn/NiTi film electrode.  These shape memory alloys can also combine with other high-capacity metallic anodes, such as Si, Sb, Al, and improve their cycle performance.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp_0897z1Z4Z7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljoKM57vb3stw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhtVehs7jJ&amp;md5=926a389937e92d113086b736ffe497eb</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref64"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref64'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">64</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit64" data-doi="10.1016/j.jallcom.2012.12.085"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, X.                 10.1016/j.jallcom.2012.12.085</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D556%26publication_year%3D2013%26pages%3D86-93%26journal%3DJ.%2BAlloys%2BCompd.%26author%3DX.%2BC.%2BHe%26author%3DH.%2BL.%2BShen%26author%3DW.%2BWang%26author%3DZ.%2BZ.%2BWang%26author%3DB.%2BS.%2BZhang%26author%3DX.%2BQ.%2BLi%26title%3DThe%2BMechanical%2Band%2BThermo-Physical%2BProperties%2Band%2BElectronic%2BStructures%2Bof%2BSnS%2Band%2BSnSe%2Bin%2BOrthorhombic%2BStructure%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.jallcom.2012.12.085&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.9b12204&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.jallcom.2012.12.085&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit64R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">64</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The mechanical and thermo-physical properties and electronic structures of SnS and SnSe in orthorhombic structure</span></div><div class="casAuthors">He, Xiancong; Shen, Honglie; Wang, Wei; Wang, Zhangzhong; Zhang, Baosen; Li, Xiaoquan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Alloys and Compounds</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">556</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">86-93</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JALCEU</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The mech., thermo-phys. properties, and electronic band structures of SnS and SnSe were calcd. using d.-functional theory (DFT).  The elastic consts. of SnS and SnSe obtained from calcns. meet their mech. stability conditions.  The shear elastic properties of the {0 0 1} plane are both anisotropic for SnS and SnSe.  Both compds. exhibit brittle behavior due to their low B/G ratio.  The values of thermal capacity are close to 200 J/mol K at about 300 K, and the thermal expansion coeffs. decrease with increasing pressure at same temp.  The entropy is variable by power-exponent, and the internal energy is almost linear with increasing temp. for SnS and SnSe.  The Gibbs energy of SnS is lower than that of SnSe under same temp. and pressure.  Debye temps. are 218 K and 215 K, and Grueneisen parameters are 3.25 and 2.98 for SnS and SnSe at 300 K, resp.  Main anion (S, Se)p orbitals and a few cation (Sn)p and cation (Sn)s orbitals make the major contribution to the formation of the top of the valence band, and a broad structure as the conduction band, mainly due to both the cation (Sn)p and the anion (S, Se)p.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqHubyGfcC4qbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljoKM57vb3stw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXivVKhsL8%253D&amp;md5=c2f3874d2c4659e8e919871679e5d8dd</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref65"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref65'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">65</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit65" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemmater.7b00279"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Aydemir, U.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wood, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Goddard, W. J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Snyder, G. J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Ideal Strength and Deformation Mechanism in High-Efficiency Thermoelectric SnSe</span>. <i>Chem.                 10.1021/acs.chemmater.7b00279</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D29%26publication_year%3D2017%26pages%3D2382-2389%26journal%3DChem.%2BMater.%26author%3DG.%2BLi%26author%3DU.%2BAydemir%26author%3DM.%2BWood%26author%3DW.%2BA.%2BGoddard%26author%3DP.%2BC.%2BZhai%26author%3DQ.%2BJ.%2BZhang%26author%3DG.%2BJ.%2BSnyder%26title%3DIdeal%2BStrength%2Band%2BDeformation%2BMechanism%2Bin%2BHigh-Efficiency%2BThermoelectric%2BSnSe%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facs.chemmater.7b00279&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.9b12204&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facs.chemmater.7b00279&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit65R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">65</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Ideal strength and deformation mechanism in high-efficiency thermoelectric SnSe</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Guodong; Aydemir, Umut; Wood, Max; Goddard, William A.; Zhai, Pengcheng; Zhang, Qingjie; Snyder, G. Jeffrey</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">29</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2382-2389</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMATEX</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0897-4756</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The widespread use of thermoelec. conversion technol. requires thermoelec. materials of high thermoelec. efficiency and high fracture strength.  Single crystal SnSe shows an extremely high zT value in the moderate temp. range, but its mech. properties have rarely been studied so far.  Here, the authors use d. functional theory to det. the ideal strength and deformation mechanism of perfect SnSe single crystals for shear deformations.  The lowest ideal strength of SnSe is found to be 0.59 GPa under the (100)/〈001〉 shear load, which agrees with exptl. evidence that single crystals cleave along the (100) slip plane.  The van der Waals-like Se-Sn bond, which couples the different Se-Sn layered substructures, is much softer than the covalent Se-Sn bond which constructs the Se-Sn layered substructure.  This creates pathways of easy slip between Se-Sn layered substructures, which can release shear stress and lead to structural failure.  Meanwhile, the layered substructures themselves can resist shearing within the (100)/〈001〉 slip system.  These results provide a plausible at. explanation for understanding the intrinsic mechanics of SnSe.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq64Q4LeBzecLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljoKM57vb3stw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXjtVajtLo%253D&amp;md5=7d9d1b778a5cc663a3033d9b735c16ce</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref66"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref66'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">66</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit66" data-doi="10.1126/science.1157996"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, X.                 10.1126/science.1157996</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D321%26publication_year%3D2008%26pages%3D385-388%26journal%3DScience%26author%3DC.%2BLee%26author%3DX.%2BD.%2BWei%26author%3DJ.%2BW.%2BKysar%26author%3DJ.%2BHone%26title%3DMeasurement%2Bof%2BThe%2BElastic%2BProperties%2Band%2BIntrinsic%2BStrength%2Bof%2BMonolayer%2BGraphene%26doi%3D10.1126%252Fscience.1157996&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.9b12204&amp;doiOfLink=10.1126%2Fscience.1157996&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit66R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">66</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Measurement of the Elastic Properties and Intrinsic Strength of Monolayer Graphene</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lee, Changgu; Wei, Xiaoding; Kysar, Jeffrey W.; Hone, James</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Science (Washington, DC, United States)</span>

        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5887</span>),

        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0036-8075</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Association for the Advancement of Science</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">We measured the elastic properties and intrinsic breaking strength of free-standing monolayer graphene membranes by nanoindentation in an at. force microscope.  The force-displacement behavior is interpreted within a framework of nonlinear elastic stress-strain response, and yields second- and third-order elastic stiffnesses of 340 newtons per m (N m-1) and -690 N m-1, resp.  The breaking strength is 42 N m-1 and represents the intrinsic strength of a defect-free sheet.  These quantities correspond to a Young's modulus of E = 1.0 terapascals, third-order elastic stiffness of D = -2.0 terapascals, and intrinsic strength of σint = 130 gigapascals for bulk graphite.  These expts. establish graphene as the strongest material ever measured, and show that atomically perfect nanoscale materials can be mech. tested to deformations well beyond the linear regime.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGos0ravf_p_AbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljoKM57vb3stw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD1cXosVOrs7k%253D&amp;md5=c85babfb5827a1ce93f7e9673a4c8b86</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref67"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref67'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">67</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit67" data-doi="10.1016/j.commatsci.2011.10.032"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, M.                         cas_cit67R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">67</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Effect of defects on fracture strength of graphene sheets</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, M.         (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">54</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),

                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMMSEM</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">With a hexagonal monolayer network of carbon atoms, graphene has demonstrated exceptional elec. and mech. properties.  In this work, the fracture of graphene sheets with Stone-Wales type defects and vacancies were investigated using mol. dynamics simulations at different temps.  The initiation of defects via bond rotation was also investigated.  The results indicate that the defects and vacancies can cause significant strength loss in graphene.  The fracture strength of graphene is also affected by temp. and loading directions.  The simulation results were compared with the prediction from the quantized fracture mechanics.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review of battery, fuel cell, and electrochem. capacitor technologies.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpmNTvGdPgVJLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljaYLOaoXBAzw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD2cXnvVKltrg%253D&amp;md5=cc4278404126e50a1723d5768a20fc7d</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1103/PhysRevB.32.2538"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">DiVincenzo, D. P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mele, E. J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Cohesion and Structure in Stage-1 Graphite Intercalation Compounds</span>. <i>Phys. Rev. B</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">1985</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>32</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">2538</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">2553</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1103/PhysRevB.32.2538</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Cohesion and structure in stage-1 graphite intercalation compounds</span></div><div class="casAuthors">DiVincenzo, D. P.; Mele, E. J.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Physical Review B:  Condensed Matter and Materials Physics</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1985</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">32</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2538-53</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">PRBMDO</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0163-1829</span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">A Thomas-Fermi theory is developed for the structural and elastic properties of the 1st-stage alkali-metal graphite intercalation compds.  A simplified model is used for the electronic structure of these materials which assumes full charge transfer between the alkali metal and the graphite, no hybridization between metal and C states, and a uniform distribution of the donated charge on the graphite planes.  Lattice consts., compressibilities, shear moduli, alkali-metal diffusion consts. and activation energies, and domain-wall thicknesses were calcd. and generally agree with available expts.  The model of the electronic properties is consistent with known elastic and structural properties.  The interplane metal-C interaction is mostly detd. by a competition between Coulomb attraction and hard-core repulsion.  The Li ion is much more compact than that of K, Rb, or Cs, which explains the higher compressibility of the Li graphite interaction compd. and the lower Li ionic mobility.  The Na-C bond is very soft, explaining the lack of formation of Na-intercalated graphite.  The in-plane alkali-metal-alkali-metal interaction is detd. almost entirely by the Coulomb interaction, and is thus relatively independent of the alkali-metal species.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpdQAgOFCEOHbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljaYLOaoXBAzw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaL2MXltlaksL4%253D&amp;md5=f6eb757c0f1189d13511507ff0a1608f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref3" class="rightTabRefNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit3" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms5033"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wen, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Y. J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, F. D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, Y. H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Matsuda, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ishii, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cumings, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C. Commun.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2014</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>5</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">5033</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                            rightTab-cas_R
                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref4" class="rightTabRefNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit4" data-doi="10.1016/S0013-4686(02)00250-5"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Thomas, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Billaud, D.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical Insertion of Sodium into Hard Carbons</span>. <i>Electrochim. Acta</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2002</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>47</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">3303</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">3307</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/S0013-4686(02)00250-5</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical insertion of sodium into hard carbons</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Thomas, P.; Billaud, D.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2002</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">47</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">20</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3303-3307</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ELCAAV</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-4686</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Science Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. insertion of sodium ions into different types of hard carbons was achieved in electrolytes composed of ethylene carbonate as the solvent and NaClO4 as the salt.  For all the materials studied the sodium uptake increases when the carbon highest heat treatment temp. (HTT) decreases.  PAN-based carbon fibers appear to be suitable structures to allow significant sodium insertion.  Thus, T650 ex-PAN fibers lead to a reversible capacity close to 209 mAh g-1.  In that case, sodium insertion occurs in two main ways: one is the adsorption on the single graphene layers and the other is the concomitant insertion into the porosity that occurs below 0.1 V vs. Na+/Na.  This second mechanism, which is indicated by a low-voltage plateau on the electrochem. curves, allows significant insertion.  The compared electrochem. study of two saccharose-coke samples corresponding to different regions of Dahn's classification underlines the importance of the carbon precursor and of the manuf. process.  The reversible capacity is equal to 184 mAh g-1 for the sample heat treated at 800 °C which presents a high hydrogen content whereas it is close to 145 mAh g-1 for the one characterized by a HTT of about 1500 °C and a low hydrogen content.  The best electrochem. performances are obtained for pyrolyzed cellulose carbons.  Indeed, the reversible capacity is about 279 mAh g-1.  Outgassing these carbons at 950°C results in such a decrease of the reversible capacity down to 145 mAh g-1.  That can be related either to the thermal elimination of heteroelements or to modifications of the pore size distribution.  Consequently, the most suitable hard carbon material for anodic applications in rechargeable sodium-ion batteries should both present a high residual hydrogen content and a significant microporosity.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpJDGOzzH5pW7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljaYLOaoXBAzw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD38Xlslylsrg%253D&amp;md5=54dfbedb15fd432f5c3abc03b64cd1a2</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref5" class="rightTabRefNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit5" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201100854"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Murata, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ishikawa, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yabuuchi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ozeki, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nakayama, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ogata, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gotoh, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fujiwara, K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical Na Insertion and Solid Electrolyte Interphase for Hard-Carbon Electrodes and Application to Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Recently, lithium-ion batteries were attracting more interest for use in automotive applications.  Lithium resources are confirmed to be unevenly distributed in South America, and the cost of the lithium raw materials has roughly doubled from the first practical application in 1991 to the present and is increasing due to global demand for lithium-ion accumulators.  Since the electrochem. equiv. and std. potential of sodium are the most advantageous after lithium, sodium based energy storage is of great interest to realize lithium-free high energy and high voltage batteries.  However, to the best of authors knowledge, there were no successful reports on electrochem. sodium insertion materials for battery applications; the major challenge is the neg. electrode and its passivation.  In this study, high capacity and excellent reversibility are achieved of sodium-insertion performance of hard-carbon and layered NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 electrodes in propylene carbonate electrolyte solns.  The structural change and passivation for hard-carbon are investigated to study the reversible sodium insertion.  The 3-V secondary Na-ion battery possessing environmental and cost friendliness, Na+-shuttlecock hard-carbon/NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 cell, demonstrates steady cycling performance as next generation secondary batteries and an alternative to Li-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrp5J70QNujrrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljaYLOaoXBAzw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXhtVWjurfM&amp;md5=be5cae72214fa773ada0e3fcca7b49d1</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref6" class="rightTabRefNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit6" data-doi="10.1016/j.carbon.2013.01.064"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries are promising elec. energy storage systems for sustainable energy storage applications due to the abundance of Na and their low cost.  The electrochem. properties of Na ion storage in reduced graphene oxide (RGO) were studied in an electrolyte consisting of 1 M NaClO4 in propylene carbonate (PC).  The RGO anode allowed significant Na ion insertion, leading to higher capacity at high c.d. compared to the previously reported results for C materials.  This is due to the fact that RGO possesses higher elec. cond. and is a more active host, with large interlayer distances and a disordered structure, enabling it to store more Na ions.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Pure micrometric antimony can be successfully used as neg. electrode material in Na-ion batteries, sustaining a capacity close to 600 mAh g-1 at a high rate with a Coulombic efficiency of 99 over 160 cycles, an extremely high capacity compared to any other compd. tested against both Li and Na.  The reaction mechanism with Na does not simply go through the alloying mechanism obsd. for Li where the intermediate species are those expected from the phase diagram.  In the case of Na, the intermediate phases are mostly amorphous and could not be precisely identified.  Surprisingly, it is evidenced that a competition takes place at the end of the discharge of the Sb/Na cell between the formation of the hexagonal and the cubic polymorphs of Na3Sb, the last being described in the literature as unstable at atm. pressure and only synthesized under high pressure (1-9 GPa).         </div><div class="casAbstract">Alloy anodes have shown great potential for next-generation lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) and sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  However, these applications are still limited by inherent huge vol. changes and sluggish kinetics.  To overcome such limitations, graphene-protected 3D Sb-based anodes grown on conductive substrate are designed and fabricated by a facile electrostatic-assembling and subsequent confinement replacement strategy.  As binder-free anodes for LIBs, the obtained electrode exhibits reversible capacities of 442 mAh/g at 100 mA/g and 295 mAh/g at 1000 mA/g, and a capacity retention of above 90% (based on the 10th cycle) after 200 cycles at 500 mA/g. Furthermore, the 3D architecture retains good structural integrity after cycling, confirming that the introduction of high-stretchy and robust graphene layers can effectively buffer alloying anodes, and simultaneously provide sustainable contact and protection of the active materials.                         rightTab-cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Template-Free Electrochemical Synthesis of Sn Nanofibers as High-Performance Anode Materials for Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Nam, Do-Hwan; Kim, Tae-Hee; Hong, Kyung-Sik; Kwon, Hyuk-Sang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sn nanofibers with a high aspect ratio are successfully synthesized using a simple electrodeposition process from an aq. soln. without the use of templates.  The synthesis approach involves the rapid electrochem. deposition of Sn accompanied by the strong adsorption of Triton X-100, which can function as a growth modifier for the Sn crystallites.  Triton X-100 is adsorbed on the {200} crystallog. planes of Sn in an elongated configuration and suppressed the preferential growth of Sn along the [100] direction.  Consequently, the Sn electrodeposits are forced to grow anisotropically in a direction normal to the (112) or (‾112) plane, forming one-dimensional nanofibers.  As electrode materials for the Na-ion batteries, the Sn nanofibers exhibit a high reversible capacity and an excellent cycle performance; the charge capacity is maintained at 776.26 mA-h/g after 100 cycles, which corresponds to a retention of 95.09% of the initial charge capacity.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. performance of mesoporous carbon (C)/tin (Sn) anodes in Na-ion and Li-ion batteries is systematically investigated.  The mesoporous C/Sn anodes in a Na-ion battery shows similar cycling stability but lower capacity and poorer rate capability than that in a Li-ion battery.  The desodiation potentials of Sn anodes are approx. 0.21 V lower than delithiation potentials.  The low capacity and poor rate capability of C/Sn anode in Na-ion batteries is mainly due to the large Na-ion size, resulting in slow Na-ion diffusion and large vol. change of porous C/Sn composite anode during alloy/dealloy reactions.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A new type of hierarchical tin@carbon composite composed of graphene carbonaceous matrix and well-confined tin nanoparticles with a typical size of ∼15 nm (denoted as F-G/Sn@C) is developed and investigated as anode material for Li-ion and Na-ion storage.  The two dimensional graphene backboned matrix not only acts as a confinement layer preventing the tin nanoparticles from aggregating during the material prepn., but also functions as a phys. barrier to buffer the vol. change effect during charge/discharge processes.  As a consequence, the composite demonstrates excellent rate performance and cycling stability for both Li-ion and Na-ion storage.  In particular, the F-G/Sn@C anode exhibits an impressive reversible capacity of 506 mAh g-1 even after 500 cycles and a high-rate capacity of 270 mAh g-1 even at 3200 mA g-1 for Li-ion storage.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries have been considered as promising alternatives to Li-ion batteries due to the natural abundance of sodium resources.  Searching for high-performance anode materials currently becomes a hot topic and also a great challenge for developing Na-ion batteries.  In this work, a novel hybrid anode is synthesized consisting of ultrafine, few-layered SnS2 anchored on few-layered reduced graphene oxide (rGO) by a facile solvothermal route.  The SnS2/rGO hybrid exhibits a high capacity, ultralong cycle life, and superior rate capability.  The hybrid can deliver a high charge capacity of 649 mA-h/g at 100 mA/g. The hybrid can also sustain a c.d. up to 12.8 A/g (≈28C) where the charge process can be completed in only 1.3 min while still delivering a charge capacity of 337 mA-h/g.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The first-ever study of nickel selenide materials as efficient anode materials for Na-ion rechargeable batteries is conducted using the electrospinning process.  NiSe2-reduced graphene oxide (rGO)-C composite nanofibers are successfully prepd. via electrospinning and a subsequent selenization process.  The electrospun nanofibers giving rise to these porous-structured composite nanofibers with optimum amt. of amorphous C are obtained from the polystyrene to polyacrylonitrile ratio of 1/4.  These composite nanofibers also consist of uniformly distributed single-cryst. NiSe2 nanocrystals that have a mean size of 27 nm.  In contrast, the densely structured bare NiSe2 nanofibers formed via selenization of the pure NiO nanofibers consist of large crystallites.  The initial discharge capacities of the NiSe2-rGO-C composite and bare NiSe2 nanofibers at a c.d. of 200 mA g-1 are 717 and 755 mA h g-1, resp.  However, the resp. 100th-cycle discharge capacities of the former and latter are 468 and 35 mA h g-1.  Electrochem. impedance spectroscopy measurements reveal the structural stability of the composite nanofibers during repeated Na-ion insertion and extn. processes.         </div><div class="casAbstract">SnSe nanoplates with thin and uniform morphol. are prepd. by one-pot spray pyrolysis, and are examd. as anode materials for Na-ion batteries. Metallic Sn and metalloid Se, with m.ps. of 232 and 221 °C, resp., form a melted Sn-Se mixt., which reacts exothermally to form SnSe nanocrystals.  Several of these nanocrystals are grown simultaneously forming a micron-sized powder.  Complete elimination of the excess amt. of metalloid Se, by forming H2Se gas, results in aggregation-free SnSe nanoplates.  The aspect ratio of these nanoplates is as high as 11.3.  The discharge capacities for the SnSe nanoplates, prepd. from spray solns. contg. 100, 400, and 800% of the stoichiometric SeO2 content needed to form SnSe, are 407, 558, and 211 mA h g-1, resp., after 50 cycles at a const. c.d. of 0.3 A g-1; their capacity retentions calcd. from the second cycle onwards are 77, 100, and 60%, resp.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Silicon is an attractive material for anodes in energy storage devices, because it has ten times the theor. capacity of its state-of-the-art carbonaceous counterpart.  Silicon anodes can be used both in traditional lithium-ion batteries and in more recent Li-O2 and Li-S batteries as a replacement for the dendrite-forming lithium metal anodes.  The main challenges assocd. with silicon anodes are structural degrdn. and instability of the solid-electrolyte interphase caused by the large vol. change (∼300%) during cycling, the occurrence of side reactions with the electrolyte, and the low volumetric capacity when the material size is reduced to a nanometer scale.  Here, we propose a hierarchical structured silicon anode that tackles all three of these problems.  Our design is inspired by the structure of a pomegranate, where single silicon nanoparticles are encapsulated by a conductive carbon layer that leaves enough room for expansion and contraction following lithiation and delithiation.  An ensemble of these hybrid nanoparticles is then encapsulated by a thicker carbon layer in micrometre-size pouches to act as an electrolyte barrier.  As a result of this hierarchical arrangement, the solid-electrolyte interphase remains stable and spatially confined, resulting in superior cyclability (97% capacity retention after 1,000 cycles).  In addn., the microstructures lower the electrode-electrolyte contact area, resulting in high Coulombic efficiency (99.87%) and volumetric capacity (1,270 mAh cm-3), and the cycling remains stable even when the areal capacity is increased to the level of com. lithium-ion batteries (3.7 mAh cm-2).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrDypwI__SZwLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj1mLDj-8h-bg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXisFOnsL0%253D&amp;md5=bdea36fbc75cb06460488082beaea1e7</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref31"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref31" class="rightTabRefNumLink">31</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit31" data-doi="10.1016/S1006-7191(06)62081-4"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dai, L.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Ni2FeGa is a relatively new shape memory alloy (SMA) and exhibits superior characteristics compared to other SMAs.  Its favorable properties include low transformation stress, high reversible strains and small hysteresis.  The first stage of stress-induced martensitic transformation is from a cubic to a modulated monoclinic phase.  The energy barriers assocd. with the transformation from L21 (cubic) to modulated martensite (10M-martensite) incorporating shear and shuffle are established via atomistic simulations.  In addn., the slip resistance in the [111] direction and the dissocn. of full dislocations into partials as well as slip in the [001] direction are studied.  The unstable stacking fault energy barriers for slip by far exceeded the transformation transition state barrier permitting transformation to occur with little irreversibility.  Expts. at the meso-scale on single crystals and transmission electron microscopy were conducted to provide further proof of the pseudoelastic (reversible) behavior and the presence of anti-phase boundaries.  The results have implications for design of new shape memory alloys that possess low energy barriers for transformation coupled with high barriers for dislocation slip.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrNaaLZlBioybVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhLfUWbBDXNgw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhsFGit7zL&amp;md5=f865569162ce579168fe7df2b1728858</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref33"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref33" class="rightTabRefNumLink">33</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit33" data-doi="10.1039/c2cp42767b"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Farra, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wrabetz, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Schuster, M. Here we characterize ceria in its fresh and post-reaction states by adsorption of CO2, NH3 and CO.  Micro-calorimetry, FTIR and TPD expts. are complemented by DFT calcns., which assess adsorption energies and vibrational frequencies.  The calcns. were performed on the lowest energy surface, CeO2(111), with perfect termination and with various degrees of hydroxylation and/or chlorination. In this paper, it is shown that the org. cations, i.e., FA and MA, play a vital role in the defect properties of Sn halide perovskites. Visible Raman data on disordered, amorphous, and diamondlike C are classified in a 3-stage model to show the factors that control the position, intensity, and widths of the G and D peaks. In cases where the sp2 clustering is controlled by the sp3 fraction, such as in as-deposited tetrahedral amorphous C (ta-C) or hydrogenated amorphous C (a-C:H) films, the visible Raman parameters can be used to derive the sp3 fraction.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqqdAvFvqG2KrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhLfUWbBDXNgw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD3cXjs1Smu7c%253D&amp;md5=e451e6f21e1f6cf375931e6a23e836bb</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref39"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref39" class="rightTabRefNumLink">39</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit39" data-doi="10.1021/acsenergylett.8b00303"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Q. Here, we construct a pyridinic-N-dominated doped graphene with abundant vacancy defects.  The optimized sample with an ultrahigh pore vol.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The effects of mech. milling on the microstructure, morphol. and electrochem. performance of graphite powders with respect to lithium insertion are studied.  After 150 h of ball-milling, the well-graphitized graphite has been pulverized into small particles with a size of about 50 nm, in which there are a lot of excess vacancies, microcavities and metastable carbon interstitial phases with sizes around 13 A.  Due to the large surface energy, the merging of single particles is favored and results in the formation of agglomerates with av. size about 1 μm.  Voids are formed among the agglomerated particles.  The ball-milled graphite shows reversible specific capacity for lithium of 700 mA h g-1 (L1.88C6) with large hysteresis.  The large reversible capacity is due mainly to Li doping at vacancies, microcavities (or at the edges of the metastable carbon interstitial phase) and voids.  The bonding change between the interstitial carbon and the carbon in the arom. plane that is induced by insertion of Li atoms leads to hysteresis.  During charge-discharge cycles, the reversible capacity above 1 V decreases rapidly, which may be due to some vacancies and microcavities being annihilated by moveable and some bound interstitial carbon and to electrolyte penetrating gradually into voids formed by agglomerated particles during the Li insertion and desertion process.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpSI-V2zWLiHLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhWZzXI7zzkcA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaK1cXntVGgu7Y%253D&amp;md5=8a4b99d338a17feda7c65133e0a7735f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref41"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref41" class="rightTabRefNumLink">41</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit41" data-doi="10.1002/jrs.2321"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Recent Raman scattering studies in different types of graphene samples are reviewed here.  We first discuss the first-order and the double resonance Raman scattering mechanisms in graphene, which give rise to the most prominent Raman features.  The detn. of the no. of layers in few-layer graphene is discussed, giving special emphasis to the possibility of using Raman spectroscopy to distinguish a monolayer from few-layer graphene stacked in the Bernal (AB) configuration.  Different types of graphene samples produced both by exfoliation and using epitaxial methods are described and their Raman spectra are compared with those of 3D cryst. graphite and turbostratic graphite, in which the layers are stacked with rotational disorder.  We show that Resonance Raman studies, where the energy of the excitation laser line can be tuned continuously, can be used to probe electrons and phonons near the Dirac point of graphene and, in particular allowing a detn. to be made of the tight-binding parameters for bilayer graphene.  The special process of electron-phonon interaction that renormalizes the phonon energy giving rise to the Kohn anomaly is discussed, and is illustrated by gated expts. where the position of the Fermi level can be changed exptl.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Anisotropic materials are of considerable interest because of their unique combination of polarization- or direction-dependent elec., optical, and thermoelec. properties.  Low-symmetry two-dimensional (2D) materials formed by van der Waals stacking of covalently bonded at. layers are inherently anisotropic.  Layered SnSe exhibits a low degree of lattice symmetry, with a distorted NaCl structure and an in-plane anisotropy.  Here we report a systematic study of the in-plane anisotropic properties in layered SnSe, using angle-resolved Raman scattering, optical absorption, and elec. transport studies.  The optical and elec. characterization was direction-dependent, and successfully identified the cryst. orientation in the layered SnSe.  Furthermore, the dependence of Raman-intensity anisotropy on the SnSe flake thickness and the excitation wavelength were investigated by both expts. and theor. calcns.  Finally, the elec. transport studies demonstrated that few-layer SnSe field-effect transistors (FETs) have a large anisotropic ratio of carrier mobility (∼5.8) between the armchair and zigzag directions, which is a record high value reported for 2D anisotropic materials.  The highly-anisotropic properties of layered SnSe indicate considerable promise for anisotropic optics, electronics, and optoelectronics.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGocsiLF5-Tcb7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhWZzXI7zzkcA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhtlWhtL%252FL&amp;md5=bb515b07e3af3939a4cfbc1ae80ff072</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref46"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref46" class="rightTabRefNumLink">46</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit46" data-doi="10.1039/C8DT00285A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Khan, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Li-Se batteries, using Se as a cathode material, exhibit some notable advantages, such as high discharge rates and good cycling performance, due to their high elec. cond., high output voltages, and high volumetric capacity d. Herein, we report SnSe nanoplates vertically grown on nitrogen-doped carbon (SnSe/NC) with strong Sn-C bonding for high-performance anode in SIBs, which are synthesized by a facial cation-exchange strategy.  The vertically grown SnSe nanoplates with large interplanar spacing (0.58 nm), strong electronic coupling of Sn-C bonding between SnSe and NC facilitate the fast electron/ion transfer, enabling high pseudocapacitive Na-ion storage and enhanced power capability.  D. functional theory calcns. demonstrate that the presence of N heteroatoms in NC matrix has a pivotal influence on the formation of Sn-C bonding to stabilize the SnSe/NC structure.                         rightTab-cas_cit52R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">52</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Crystal Orbital Hamilton Population (COHP) Analysis As Projected from Plane-Wave Basis Sets</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Deringer, Volker L.; Tchougreeff, Andrei L.; Dronskowski, Richard</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry A</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2011</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Simple, yet predictive bonding models are essential achievements of chem.  In the solid state, in particular, they often appear in the form of visual bonding indicators.  Because the latter require the crystal orbitals to be constructed from local basis sets, the application of the most popular d.-functional theory codes (namely, those based on plane waves and pseudopotentials) appears as being ill-fitted to retrieve the chem. bonding information.  In this paper, we describe a way to re-ext. Hamilton-weighted populations from plane-wave electronic-structure calcns. to develop a tool analogous to the familiar crystal orbital Hamilton population (COHP) method.  We derive the new technique, dubbed "projected COHP" (pCOHP), and demonstrate its viability using examples of covalent, ionic, and metallic crystals (diamond, GaAs, CsCl, and Na).  For the first time, this chem. bonding information is directly extd. from the results of plane-wave calcns.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqJK3R7pxAxX7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhEhwjTLxSHdw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXlslKitr8%253D&amp;md5=4b8024fd1ed30e78710deaaf6d0d3796</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref53"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref53" class="rightTabRefNumLink">53</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit53" data-doi="10.1103/PhysRevB.76.214432"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bellini, V.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Di Giustino, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Manghi, F.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Ab Initio Study of The Fe/NiO Interface: Structural and Magnetic Properties</span>. <i>Phys.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Pseudocapacitance is commonly assocd. with surface or near-surface reversible redox reactions, as obsd. with RuO2·xH2O in an acidic electrolyte.  However, we recently demonstrated that a pseudocapacitive mechanism occurs when lithium ions are inserted into mesoporous and nanocrystal films of orthorhombic Nb2O5 (T-Nb2O5; refs , ).  Here, we quantify the kinetics of charge storage in T-Nb2O5: currents that vary inversely with time, charge-storage capacity that is mostly independent of rate, and redox peaks that exhibit small voltage offsets even at high rates.  We also define the structural characteristics necessary for this process, termed intercalation pseudocapacitance, which are a cryst. network that offers two-dimensional transport pathways and little structural change on intercalation.  The principal benefit realized from intercalation pseudocapacitance is that high levels of charge storage are achieved within short periods of time because there are no limitations from solid-state diffusion.  Thick electrodes (up to 40 μm thick) prepd. with T-Nb2O5 offer the promise of exploiting intercalation pseudocapacitance to obtain high-rate charge-storage devices.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrPatnkXnvsNbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lijWAj_I89W1w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXlsl2jtL8%253D&amp;md5=ef16739fcf485430955b0414ac12f1ac</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref60"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref60" class="rightTabRefNumLink">60</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit60" data-doi="10.1038/nmat2612"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Brezesinski, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tolbert, S.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Capacitive energy storage is distinguished from other types of electrochem. energy storage by short charging times and the ability to deliver significantly more power than batteries.  A limitation to this technol. is its low energy d. and there is interest in exploring pseudo-capacitive materials where faradaic mechanisms offer increased energy storage.  The capacitive charge-storage properties of mesoporous films of iso-oriented α-MoO3 are superior to those of either mesoporous amorphous material or nonporous cryst. MoO3.  Whereas both cryst. and amorphous mesoporous materials show redox pseudo-capacitance, the iso-oriented layered cryst. domains enable Li ions to be inserted into the van der Waals gaps of the α-MoO3.  Probably this extra contribution arises from an intercalation pseudo-capacitance, which occurs on the same timescale as redox pseudo-capacitance.  The result is increased charge-storage capacity without compromising charge/discharge kinetics in mesoporous cryst. MoO3.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp77bOsasmjcrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lijWAj_I89W1w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3cXhtVOnt78%253D&amp;md5=b6e743b60b27d6e0dfcabcf281d3d475</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref61"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref61" class="rightTabRefNumLink">61</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit61" data-doi="10.1039/C6TA03083A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, L.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Transition metal carbides are an emerging class of anode materials for Li-ion batteries (LIBs), which have recently drawn attention because of their good cond. and high capacity after rational nano-engineering.  In this work, we have developed Mo2C/N-doped carbon mesoporous heteronanowires (Mo2C/N-C MHNWs) with enhanced capacitive behavior as high-performance anode materials for LIBs.  With the heterostructure, the Mo2C nanocrystallites offer short paths for Li+ diffusion, while the N-doped carbon matrix facilitates fast electron transportation and buffers the vol. change of Mo2C during the discharge/charge cycles.  When evaluated as anodes for LIBs, the Mo2C/N-C MHNWs exhibited high capacity and high rate capability, as well as a long-term cycle life. The outstanding performance stems from fast kinetics enhanced by the pseudocapacitive effect, which was evidenced in the further anal. based on electrochem. impedance spectra and cyclic voltammetry.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The widespread use of thermoelec. conversion technol. requires thermoelec. materials of high thermoelec. efficiency and high fracture strength.  Single crystal SnSe shows an extremely high zT value in the moderate temp. range, but its mech. properties have rarely been studied so far.  Here, the authors use d. functional theory to det. the ideal strength and deformation mechanism of perfect SnSe single crystals for shear deformations.  The lowest ideal strength of SnSe is found to be 0.59 GPa under the (100)/〈001〉 shear load, which agrees with exptl. evidence that single crystals cleave along the (100) slip plane.  The van der Waals-like Se-Sn bond, which couples the different Se-Sn layered substructures, is much softer than the covalent Se-Sn bond which constructs the Se-Sn layered substructure.  This creates pathways of easy slip between Se-Sn layered substructures, which can release shear stress and lead to structural failure.  Meanwhile, the layered substructures themselves can resist shearing within the (100)/〈001〉 slip system.  These results provide a plausible at. explanation for understanding the intrinsic mechanics of SnSe.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq64Q4LeBzecLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhotnNuvntaVQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXjtVajtLo%253D&amp;md5=7d9d1b778a5cc663a3033d9b735c16ce</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref66"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref66" class="rightTabRefNumLink">66</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit66" data-doi="10.1126/science.1157996"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, X. D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kysar, J. W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hone, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Measurement of The Elastic Properties and Intrinsic Strength of Monolayer Graphene</span>. <i>Science</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2008</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>321</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">385</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">388</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1126/science.1157996</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit66R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">66</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Measurement of the Elastic Properties and Intrinsic Strength of Monolayer Graphene</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lee, Changgu; Wei, Xiaoding; Kysar, Jeffrey W.; Hone, James</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Science (Washington, DC, United States)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2008</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">321</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5887</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">385-388</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">SCIEAS</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0036-8075</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Association for the Advancement of Science</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">We measured the elastic properties and intrinsic breaking strength of free-standing monolayer graphene membranes by nanoindentation in an at. force microscope.  The force-displacement behavior is interpreted within a framework of nonlinear elastic stress-strain response, and yields second- and third-order elastic stiffnesses of 340 newtons per m (N m-1) and -690 N m-1, resp.  The breaking strength is 42 N m-1 and represents the intrinsic strength of a defect-free sheet.  These quantities correspond to a Young's modulus of E = 1.0 terapascals, third-order elastic stiffness of D = -2.0 terapascals, and intrinsic strength of σint = 130 gigapascals for bulk graphite.  These expts. establish graphene as the strongest material ever measured, and show that atomically perfect nanoscale materials can be mech. tested to deformations well beyond the linear regime.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGos0ravf_p_AbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhotnNuvntaVQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD1cXosVOrs7k%253D&amp;md5=c85babfb5827a1ce93f7e9673a4c8b86</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref67"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref67" class="rightTabRefNumLink">67</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit67" data-doi="10.1016/j.commatsci.2011.10.032"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, M. C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, M. W.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Effect of Defects on Fracture Strength of Graphene Sheets</span>. <i>Comput. Mater. Sci.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2012</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>54</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">236</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">239</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.commatsci.2011.10.032</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit67R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">67</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Effect of defects on fracture strength of graphene sheets</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, M. C.; Yan, C.; Ma, L.; Hu, N.; Chen, M. W.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Computational Materials Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">54</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">236-239</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMMSEM</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0927-0256</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">With a hexagonal monolayer network of carbon atoms, graphene has demonstrated exceptional elec. and mech. properties.  In this work, the fracture of graphene sheets with Stone-Wales type defects and vacancies were investigated using mol. dynamics simulations at different temps.  The initiation of defects via bond rotation was also investigated.  The results indicate that the defects and vacancies can cause significant strength loss in graphene.  The fracture strength of graphene is also affected by temp. and loading directions.  The simulation results were compared with the prediction from the quantized fracture mechanics.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp9Rai6DDdfFbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhotnNuvntaVQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhtVOjsrs%253D&amp;md5=99b7f99306bfc1e62968c6b1867a702b</span></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i20">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.9b12204" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.9b12204</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">SEM images of SnSe, SnSe/C, EG, Sn, and Se; HRTEM images of SnSe and SnSe/C; N<sub>2</sub> adsorption isotherms of SnSe, SnSe/C, and SnSe/FLG; XPS spectra of SnSe/C; comparison of the volumetric capacity of the SnS/FLG anode in this work with previously reported anodes for LIBs; Nyquist plots tested at the potential of 2.0 V in the initial cycle and <i>Z</i>′ vs ω plots in the low-frequency region of SnSe, SnSe/C, and SnSe/FLG; fitting results of Nyquist plots using an equivalent circuit; Nyquist plots of SnSe/FLG tested at 0.01 V after different cycles; CV curves at various scan rates and log <i>i</i> vs log <i>v</i> plots of SnSe, SnSe/C, and SnSe/FLG; CV curves at various scan rates and log <i>i</i> vs log <i>v</i> plots of SnSe/FLG after 200 cycles; elemental mapping of the SnSe/FLG electrode after 2000 cycles; XPS spectra of SnSe/FLG after 200 cycles; Raman spectra of SnSe/FLG after different cycles; cell parameters of SnSe, Li<sub>2</sub>Se, and Li<sub>22</sub>Sn<sub>5</sub>; comparison of the cyclic stability of SnSe/FLG and recently reported Sn-based anodes for SIBs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b12204/suppl_file/am9b12204_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b12204/suppl_file/am9b12204_si_001.pdf">am9b12204_si_001.pdf (1.47 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div><div id="rightTab-figshare-widget"></div><br><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="b84e6b6c-b4d0-4427-b286-3cf14d52d013" class="mb-4">

(function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	}); 

	$('.share_button').on('click', function(e){
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

	$("#pane-m1con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Figures'});
	});

	$("#pane-m2con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'References'});
	});

	$("#pane-m3con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Supporting Info'});
	});

	$('.tab__close').on('click', function(){
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Close'});
	}); 



})();

<script nonce="90da91a74c29b325-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da91a74c29b325-SJC">
"use strict"
//Script checks gdpr cookie and submits form
	/* 
	Create an in-page pop-up message, only for the InfoCentral homepage, that will gather email registrants for the library marketing list.
	 
	Needs the end point
	*/

var infoCentralCapture = (function() {

    //Detect mobile device
    window.mobilecheck = function() {
        var check = false;
        (function(a){if(/(android|bb\d+|meego).+mobile|avantgo|bada\/|blackberry|blazer|compal|elaine|fennec|hiptop|iemobile|ip(hone|od)|iris|kindle|lge |maemo|midp|mmp|mobile.+firefox|netfront|opera
